Special Dispatch to The Call.
In view of the fact that Congress and
not the executive is empowered to deter
mine whether an officer of the Govern
ment shall receive a decoration from a for
eign Government, the decision of the
President will undoubtedly cause a stir
In the Capitol. Both the admiral and the
general have many friends in the Senate
end the House who will be inclined to
take up the matter and press It if allowed
to do so.
"There was absolutely no justification
for the President's decision." said a friend
of General Miles. "Mr. Roosevelt has at
tempted to Inflict national disgrace upon
the commanding general of the army and
the admiral of the navy, and now' he has
sought to make their humiliation Inter
national. Of course it will be Impossible
for General Miles to take any action, but
his friends will now understand the
lengths to which the President is willing
to go In his hostility toward him."
Admiral Dewey is inspecting the North
Atlantic squadron on - the southern drill
grounds. General Miles refused to be
seen this afternoon In regard to the pro
posal of the French Government and its
rejection by the President. It is stated,
however, that neither officer win make a
protest. Nor is it seen how this could be
done, in view of the fact that the French
Government never directly tendered the
decoration to them.
FURTHER HUMILIATION.
A notification that the cross would be
conferred upon Admiral Dewey and Lieu
tenant General Miles was taken to the
White House by M. Cambon, the French
Embassador. France desired to commem
orate the ceremonies connected with the
unveiling of the Rochambeau statue to
occur here on May 24. and she believed it
would be a graceful act of courtesy to
select prominent Americans for member
ship of her Legion of Honor. M. Cam
bon called at the White House two weeks
ago and stated that his Government, in
consideration of the great services ren
dered to the world by Admiral Dewey
and General Miles, desired to confer the
cro^F upon them.
WOULD CONFER CROSS.
This action has caused a sensation in
diplomatic and r.nny and navy circles..
The officers directly concerned and their
friends assert that the President's de
cision was due tD a wish to further hu
miliate and this time to make the humil
iation international. Whatever may have
been the reasons controlling 1 the action of
the President, it is certain that France
will not. In view of his attitude, award
the cross to either of the officers.

Other speakers were Miss L. J. Martin
of the same university, on "Art for Chil
dren From the Standpoint of Psychol
ogy,"- and &Irs\ Lillian .Duncanson of Chi
cago, on "Fads in Education."
â¢A final question: Doe not coeducation lead
to marriaKe? 'Most certainly It does, and
this fact cannot be and need not be denied.
The wonder 4s rather .that there, are not more
of such marriare?.. It is a constant surprise
that. so many colleee men turn from their-col
lege associates '. and marry some earlier or
later acquaintance of inferior 'ability. " In
ferior traininz and often inferior; personal
charm- The marriages which result from col
lege association are not usually premature,
and It is certainly true that no better mar
riages can be made than those founded on
common interests and Intellectual friendship.
A college man who has known collese wo
men as a rule Is not drawn to those of lower
.ideals and inferior training. His choice U
likely to be led toward 'the best he has known.
A college woman Is not led by mere propin
quity to accept the attentions of inferior men.
. In I like manner the association with wise,
sane r.nj healthy women has its value for
young men. This value has never been fully
realized, even by the strongest advocates of co
education. It raises their Ideal of womanhood
and the highest manhood must be associated
with such an ideal.
. In schools for men alone the reverse condi
tion often' obtains. The sense of reality ob
scures Jhe elements of beauty and fitness.' It
is of srreat advantage to both men and women
to meet on a plane of equality in education.
Women are brought into contact with men
who can do thlnsrs â men in whom the sense
of reality Is strong and who Â¦ have definite
view3 of life. This influence affects them for
good. â¢
In women'% education as planned for women
alone the . tendency is toward the study of
beauty and order. Literature and language
take precedence over science. The educated
woman is likely to master technique rather
than art; method rather than substance. She
may know a good deal, but she can do noth
ing. .
In this direction. I think, lies the strongest
argument fcr coeducation. This argument Is
especially cocent !n institutions in which the
Individuality of the student Is .recognized and
respected. In such schools each man by his
relation to action and realities becomes a
teacher of, women in these regards, as In other
Ways each cultivated woman is a teacher of
men. . ' .' " > â¢ . .

VANCOUVER, B. C, May 5.âFollow
ing its decision to grant 'a large subsidy
to the Canadian Pacific Navigation Com
pany's steamers on the Vancouver-Skag
way route, the Canadian Government now
proposes to take another step which will
have a far-reaching effect against .Amer
ican steamers and in favor' of Canadian
boats. It is proposed to do away with an
order made by the' Ottawa Government
three years ago, which provided that
bonded freight from Vancouver for the
Canadian Yukon might be sent to Skag
way in American vessels. This was done
to accommodate shippers In the days of
tbe northern rush, when the Canadian
boats were unable to handle all tbe trade.

May Alden Ward of Boston, president

the rules for a viva voce vote, instead of

chusetts wished the house would grant
Miss Addams Indulgence on this point
Mrs. Ward also said that Massachusetts
would have supported Mrs. Shields In her
substitute amendment had the word
"colored" been left out.

Queen "Wilhelmina, although of robust
appearance, has always been somewhat
delicate, and her health has required very
careful attention. . . -
At 10 o'clock her Majesty was reported
to be sleeping quietly, and at that hour
her condition was practlally unchanged,.
The Dutch newspapers strangely publish
nothing concerning Queen Wilftelmina'a
Illness beyond the official bulletins and
brief expressions of thankfulness that her
Majesty 6 life has been saved. It Is no
longer concealed that the operation which
was performed last night was most dan
gerous, and that for an hour the patient's
life hung by a thread, causing the most
terrible suspense. Even now. although
the Queen's physicians are exceedingly
reticent. It Is .known that her condition
remains precarious and that everything
depends upon the strength of the pa
tient's constitution. There Is reasonable
hope for her Majesty's recovery, how
ever, provided that hemorrhage is avoid
ed. It Is now said her Majesty's doctors,
being no longer embarrassed by complica
tions In their patient's condition, will be
able to adopt more efficient means in
treating the typhoid. It is also held to be
exceedingly fortunate that the mishap of
last night occurred when It did. Instead of
a fortnight ago. It would then almost
certainly have proved fatal. Great hopes
are built on the fact that the Dutclv. Pre
mier. Dr. Kuyper, was not summoned to
the palace to-night.
The Dutch public Is strongly resentful
at learning that the truth of this case has
been long withheld and that reassuring
bulletins were issued while her Majesty's
condition was known to be dangerous.
This fact engenders some suspicion, and
anxiety as to the reliability of the bulle
tins issued to-day.
"Her Majesty's fever has not increased,
and we are quietly and hopefully awaiting
the result of a good night's rest for thÂ»
Queen. There is no cause for immediate
disquietude."
A bulletin posted at 2 p. m. announced
that the condition of Queen Â¦Wilhelmina
continued to be satisfactory.
The evening passed quietly at L.00 Pal
ace, but Drs. Roessingh and Pot have re
mained at the Queen's bedside. Baron
Clifford, master of the Queen's household,
said to-night:
THE HAGUE, May 5.â "It is a disap
pointment, but the Queen* is saved." Such
Is the general feeling regarding the sad
event of yesterday evening. The extreme
seriousness of her Majesty's Illness was
closely connected with the acute infec
tious malady from wh\ch she was con
valescing, and this complication involved
imminent danger of her death. The op
eration necessitated under such conditions
was performed without difficulty and
without the serious complications which
often arise in such cases. Consequently
the intense anxiety felt throughout the
castle has been replaced to 'some degree
by confidence, since the Queen'3 symp
toms continue to indicate that the dan
ger is no longer imminent.
A bulletin issued at Castle Loo this
morning Is as follows: "Ae has been
feared, the Illness of which Queen Wil
helmlna Is suffering put an end yesterday
evening to the hopes for a happy event,
the realization whereof was expected in
September next. All things considered,
her Majesty's condition is satisfactory at
the present moment."

REDDING. May 5.â Because he was
strapped firmly to the seat of the coach,
George Rogers, driver of one of the-Sis
son and Fall River Mills stages, was un
able to leap when the stage overturned
during a runaway, and his life was
crushed out beneath the cumbrous vehi
cle. The accident occurred in the vlcinity
of- Bartle's summer resort and had no
witnesses. . .
â¢ George Rogers was driving toward Sis
son. His brother John, who drives the
other stage, passed him on the road and
they exchanged a few words. The next
morning George's overturned coach' was
foynd, with, the, driver dead beneath it.
One of the horses lay dead near by,. and;
the other was badly crippled. Apparently
the harness had broken In descending a
grade. The horses had run away and the
pole struck a pine tree at the foot of the
grade. The shock broke the horses loose
and upset the stage. Rogers was strapped
to his seat, as is customary with drivers
through the mountains, and could make
no effort to save himself. 'Â¦â¢ ;â¢:*.-.:

VICTORIA, B..C, May 5.-News was
brought by the steamer Princess May,
from Juneau and Skagway, of a tragedy
near the former port, resulting in at least
four deaths. One of the victims was
Thomas Johnson, a white prospector,
who, after working for some time at the
Tresdwell mines, was beachcombing and
selling whisky to Indians along the
northern coastx The others were George
Hansen, his wife and daughter, all Sitka
Indians, who had been living at a small
camp at Petersburg, on the Douglas Isl
and ccast.

The answer of the Northern Securities
Company is divided into two parts. The
first Is largely a denial of the petition as
respecting any charge. of conspiracy and
as respecting the purposes of the organ
ization of the Securities Company. In
stead of owning a majority of the shares
of the Great Northern or Northern Pa
cific companies, it Is stated that those who
are interested in the organization of the
Securities Company did not own within
$26,000,000 of a majority .of the Great
Northern shares and little more than one
quarter of the Northern Pacific shares.
This part jot the answer states that the
Securities X'ompany has acquired by
transfer on the Great Northern , books
about five-twelfths of that company's
stock, has negotiated for about four
twelfths of the total of such stock which
has not been transferred and as to which
it has at present no .voting power, and
has paid on account of Great Northern
and Northern Pacific ' shares purchased
over $40,000,000 in cash; that many stock
holders have not and may not sell shares,
and' that neither company by any act or
suggestion has solicited shareholders to
sell to the Securities Company.
In the second part of the answer the
purchase of the Chicago, BurliHgton and
Quincy Railway Company is taken up,
the reasons for such purchase having
been, it is alleged, "erroneously stated. in
the petition."' Attention is called to the
sparsely settled or unsettled nature of the
country ; through which the Great North
ern lines pass; the abundance of raw ma
terials to be hauled at , a low mileage

ARCHBISHOP CORRIGAH PASSES AWAY
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO. May 5.-The final
canvass of the ballots for May
Queen was completed to-night
and Miss Fay Jackson was de
clared elected to the throne. She
received 157,716 votes, while her
opponent. Miss Gertrude Plumb, received
68.609 votes. The May Queen will at once
select her robes to be worn at the corona
tion ball in the Assembly chamber of the
State Capitol next Monday night. -A num
ber of young society women will dance
the minuet at this ball and it promises to
be a brilliant opening of the festival week.
On the following day, Tuesday, the
floral pageant will take place and on this
occasion the Queen and her maids of
honor, who will come from neighboring
cities, will appear in fluffy white gar
ments, affording quite a contrast to the
velvety robes of state which will be worn
at the evening events. It is believed the
floral parade will be the finest yet at
tempted in Sacramento and there will be
many attractive equipages in line.
The Street Fair Club has under consid
eration the holding of a mardi gras
toward the close of the fair week. It is
believed that it will be a splendid drawing
card and the plan doubtless will be car
ried into execution.
The Sacramento Driving Club announces
its intention to hold a race meeting at
Agricultural Park during Street Fair
week. Many of the horses from the Oak
land racetrack are expected to appear.
The Street Fair Dog Show will be an
other attraction. It is to be held under
the auspices of the California Kennel
Club. Four well-known experts have been
chosen to Judge the dogs. They are J. J.
Lynn of Port Huron. Mich.; E. C. Plume
of San Francisco, C. N. Post of Sacra
mento and Dr. George W. Clayton of Chi
cago.
pie, but by his ecclesiastical superiors.
He took a deep interest in reformatory
and institutional work and establishments
of the greatest importance to the welfare
of the people about him soon began to
rise, as it were, by magic. He dedicated
fifty new churches and gave them pastors
and consecrated a cathedral' church. Ho
continued to be president of Seton Hall,
over which he kept a watchful eye until
1876; founded a number of religious com
munities, established a reformatory for
boys, a refuge for misguided women and
a general asylum for the orphans of his
diocese. He introduced into New Jersey
the Jesuits and Dominicans and founded
the convent for the Dominican Nuns of
the Perpetual Adoration, besides other
works too numerous to list here.
On October 1, 1880. Bishop Corrigan was
made coadjutor, with the right of succes
sion, to Cardinal McCloskey, Archbishop
of New York, under the title of Arch
bishop of Petra, and thereafter nearly all
the practical work of the archdiocese fell
Into his hands. By this time the Catholic
schools of New Jersey had increased to
150, having nearly 30,000 pupils, with 150
churches and 172 priests.
Archbishop Corrigan was then the
youngest archbishop, as he had been the
youngest bishop, of the Catholic church
in America. From the beginning of his
ecclesiastical career honors had fallen to
him In a most unusual way, but never did
they change his manner or course of con
duct from the unassuming habit that be
had adopted from the first.
In 1884 Archbishop Corrigan was sum
moned to Rome, and represented New
York in the Plenary Council, called to ad
vise the Holy Father.
On October 10, 1885, the death of Car
dinal McCloskey made Archbishop Corri
gan Metropolitan of the diocese of New
York, and by special act of courtesy he
was permitted to perform, the functions.
of his office immediately upon his acces
sion. Instead of waiting, as is the custom,'
for the pallium, which in his case did not
actually arrive until early in 1886.
Archbishop Corrigan was Â¦ a profound
scholar, and, although not a great orator,
a most agreeable preacher, impressing his
hearers without resorting to any of the
usual rhetorical means of gaining and
holding their attention. His office was
conducted in a masterful manner, though
often under conditions and circumstances
the reverse of peaceful, being not infre
quently disturbed by the most bitter and
harassing internal dissensions, through all
of which with remarkable tact and judg
ment he managed to steer his course so
as to gain tne respect even of bis oppo
nents and those who disliked his public
attitude as a member of the Catholic
hierarchy of America.
dent. Soon after he was made vice presi
dent, and !n 186S. upon the appointment of
Bishop McQuaid to the newly created spÂ»
of Rochester, Father Corrigan. though
but 2$ years of age, was appointed bv
the Archbishop to be president of the col
Jc-ge. which was one of the foremost
Catholic educational institutions in the
United States.
During the absence of Archbishop Bay
ify at the Vatican Council in 1870. Father
Ccrrigan acted as administrator and vicar
general of the diocese, and when In 1S73
Archbishop Bayley was transferred to thp
see of Baltimore, thereby becoming pri
mate of America, upon his earnest recom
mendation Pius IX appointed Father Cor
rigan Bishop of Newark.
He was consecrated May 4, 1S73, in the
old St. Patrick's Cathedral by the late
Cardinal, then Archbishop McCloskey.
Bishop Corrigan In his new office soon
manifested powers that made him ad
mired and resrected not only by his peo-

pany, which runs from Blue Lakes to San

Standard line, and it is believed that
Douglas received a large part of this cur
rent. While linemen axe not able to tell
JustTiow much electricity passed Into his
body, it is said to have been at least sev
eral thousand volts more than is re
quired to kill an ordinary man.
When Douglas received the shock he
fell back unconscious in the network of
wires. His clothing caught fire. The man
was terribly burned on his back from his
fhoulders to his hips and his face and
hands were partially cooked. He was
in an unconscious condition when rescued
find removed to the Receiving Hospital.
There he soon recovered consciousness
After his burns were treated he was re
moved to St. Luke's Hospital.
While Douglas is frightfully burned
there is a slight chance that he will re
cover.
6AN JOSE, May 5.â Several thousand
volts of electricity passed through the
body of Walter Douglas, a lineman in the
employ of the Western Union Telegraph
Company, this afternoon, and he is still
alive. For at least ten seconds he lay on
the wires, about thirty feet above ground,
his clothing^burning and his flesh cooking.
Some telephone employes who were pres
ent cut off the current-and rescued Doug
hs more dead than alive.
Thousands of Volts Pass Through
Lineman's Body, but He Still
Lives.

Frank Wadsworth of New York has
been appointed to succeed William Cor
coran Eustis as third secretary of the
United States Embassy at London. Eus
tis' resignation takes effect July 1.
Squires' selection fills the very first re
quirement laid down by the President,
namely, that the Minister to Cuba
should be a man of diplomatic experU
ence. He is a native of Canada, but was
appointed from New York. In 1894 he
Â¦was appointed second secretary of the
United States Embassy at Berlin, retir
ing in 1S97. In January. 189S, he was ap
pointed secretary of the United States,
L^gat'on at Peking, remaining there con
stantly until a few months ago, when he
returned to the United States on leave.
He had had military experience, and by
virtue of that fact was practically in
charge of the legation defenses during
the Boxer uprising. Â¦_"
General Bragg:, the new Consul General
at Havana, was born in Unadilla. N. Y.,
In 1827. He entered the Union army as
a captain in 1861. and reached the grade
of brigadier general in the regular estab
lishment. He served for some years in
congress, find was afterward appointed
United States Minister to Mexico. His
present home is in Fon du Lac. Wis. The
salary of the Consul General at Havana
Js 55000.
' Tt was announced at the State Depart
ment to-day that Joseph H. Gilder, a
relative of "Richard Watson. Gilder of
New York, has been appointed United
States dispatch agent at London, filling
the vacancy caused by the death of B. F.
Stevens. The London Dispatch Agency
handles ail the official mail of this Gov
ernment going to and coming from Eu
rope, Asia and Africa, and the agency is
the recognized rendezvous for army, na
val. diplomatic and consular officers go-
Ing to and from Europe.

Rev. Dr. Ament of Peking, China, deliv
ered an interesting talk yesterday on mis
sionary experiences in the Orient. He
said the main spirit of the Idol has-been
broken and there is a new spirit abroad
and new ideas bring strife and restless
ness. He said it was necessary to leaven
the Chinese. Rev. Dr. Prentis of the
U. S. Grant University of Tennessee and
Rev. Dr. Gregory of Santa Rosa address
ed the Methodist Episcopal ministers at
their meeting yesterday.
Ministerial Meetings.
Hansted Will Be Held.
The motion to dismiss the charge
against Fred Hansted of assault to do
grievous bodily injury upon Thomas Gax
rett was argued by Attorney Archie
Campbell for the defendant and Attorney
Peter F. Dunne for the prosecution before
Judge Cabanlss yesterday and denied. At
torney Campbell was given till this morn
ing to decide whether or not he will sub
mit his case. The trial of William E.
O'Donnell on a similar charge was then
commenced and will be continued to-mor
row afternoon.
Bummage Sale for Charity.
A rummage sale for the benefit of the
Presbyterian Orphanage and farm will
bo held at 734 Greenwich street commenc
ing May 9. The ladies having the sale in
charge have spared no -effort to make it
a success, and they earnestly appeal to
all who aie charitably inclined to send
donations of old clothing of all descrip
tions. The following committee has the
affair in charge: Mrs. P. D. Brown, Mrs.
Robert Dollar, Mrs. A. E. Kent, Mrs. S.
P. Barstow, Mrs. W. C. Ridenot, Mrs. S.
McLalne, Mrs. F. Dal ton, Mrs. A. W.
Jackson, Mrs. J. S. Webster, Mrs. J. S.
Weston and Mrs. T. P. Tlsdale.

Comte made a plea against public gam
bling and urged the passage of the or
dinances, with the understanding that
they be not enforced in harmless cases.
The vote whereby the ordinances were
defeated follows:
Ayes â Booth, Comte, Connor, Payot,' Sander
son, Wilson â 6.
Noes â Alpers, Bent, Boxton, Brandenstein,
Braunhart, Curtis, D'Ancona Eggers, Lough
ery. Lynch, McClellan, Wynn â 12.
Brandenstein said the ordinances would
prevent a man from going into a corner
grocery and playing for a 5-cent chip.
"We do not desire to impose , a burden
on the general public," said Brandenstein,
"so I would oppose any such drastic
measure as working a hardship and be
ing too sweeping. The ordinances would
prevent a man from playing cards for a
giass of beer. I would suggest that the
measures be returned to the Police Com
mission to be redrafted, with a view to
removing its oppressive features and to
cover the evil intended to be suppressed."
Braunhart held that the remedy lies in
the hands of the Police Commission, who
may revoke the licenses of any man found
prosecuting any nefarious business.
Commissioner Hutton of the Police
Board stated that the ordinances were
proposed on acount "of the frequent com
plaints against the Cafe Royal and other
places in which country people were vic
timized. Hutton said that an offlcer was
sent to a room in which a poker game
was in progress and he lost $35 in five
minutes, and he added that the offlcer is
considered a good poker player.
"The officer reported that five queens
had been dealt from one pack," said Hut
ton, "and we concluded that a man had
no chance in such games. We desire to
reach the professional sharpers by., these
ordinances."

hibiting the playing or dealing of card
games for money or becoming a visitor
to such games in places where liquors are
sold.

Your petitioner desires to establish engine

The Southern Pacific Company filed
a petition yesterday with the Board
of Supervisors for a franchise to enter the
city at a point where the southerly bound
ary line of the city and county is inter
sected by Sunnyside avenue and to run
thence in a northerly direction to the
northeast line of Sixth street, opposite
South, and to construct certain branch
lines connected with said railroad on cer
tain designated streets. The petition
says:
To regulate street railways, tracks and
cars; to compel two lines of street rail
way operated under different manage
ment to use the same street, each paying
an equal portion for the construction cf
the tracks and appurtenances used by
said railways jointly; but in no case must
two lines of street railway operated under
different managements occupy and use
the same street or tracks for a distance
of more than ten blocks consecutively; to
fix, establish and reduce the fares and
charges for transporting passengers and
goods thereon; to regulate rates of speed,
and to pass ordinances to protect the
public from danger or inconvenience in
the operation of such roads.

SUPREME COURT DECIDES
CHINESE TRANSIT CASES
The names of -the two . Chinamen .who
figured in the cases decided to-day are
Fook Yung Yo and Lee Gon Yung: Both
were on their way to Mexico, and claimed
the right under the treaty of 1894 to tran
sit, one desiring to go by rail and the oth
er merely to transship.
They were denied this right under the
provision of the treaty giving the Treas
ury Department authority to make regu
lations for control in such matters. The
Federal authorities discovered evidences
In both cases, as in those of their de
tained countrymen, that the passengers
did not intend to remain In Mexico. The
WASHINGTON, May 5.â The
Chief Justice of the j United
States Supreme Court' to-day
rendered opinions in two test
cases affecting the transit of
Chinamen through' the United States to
other countries, sustaining the govern
mental authorities in having them de
tained at San Francisco. There are said
to be forty -or fifty Chinamen who have
been thus detained in San* Francisco for
the past four or five months and who
have been very restless under the re
straint. The result of the decision will be
the deportation to China of the Chinese
detained.
PRESIDING MEMBER OF SU
PREME. COURT WHO DELIV
ERED DECISION.
The Chamber ot Commerce Is In re
ceipt of an ; Invitation from the directors
of the International Commercial and In
dustrial Congress to send a representative
to the annual meeting of that body, which
will be held at Ostend on August 26. It is
not deemed probable by the officials of
tho chamber that any representative will
be sent. . â¢
May Not Send Representative.
Chief Deputy Vogelsang regards the
capture as very important- in view of the
fact that many parties are made up in
San Francisco and other cities to hunt in
Lake County in violation of^the game
laws. He considers that- the knowledge
that the Commissioners will prosecute of
fenders will have a salutary effect on
other forgetful hunters.
After some protestation they consented
to accompany the officers to Kelseyville,
about forty-five miles distant. By ar
rangement with the deputies four of the
men entered a plea of guilty' and were
fined $25 each by Justice of the Peace
Gunn.'. The other two were allowed to. go
free with a warning.
Deputy Fish Commissioners A. F. Lea
and L. N; Kercheval made an important
capture of same law violators in Big
Squaw Valley, Lake County, on Thursday
of last week. The offenders, six in num
ber, had set up a camp in the locality
where they were apprehended," and are
said -to have been making a practice of
shooting ' deer whenever opportunity of
fered. '
The party was composed of Raymond
Hill, a stage driver; F. B. Marble and
Benjamin Milton, two prominent cattle
men of Uklah; O. Cleveland, ex-City Mar

Elizabeth R. Knowlton Is suing Joseph
Knowlton for divorce for desertion and
failure to provide. They were married at
Gilroy in June, 1870. Mrs. Knowlton al
leges that her husband is worth $5000.
Maggie Alden brought a suit for divorce
against Charles Alden, a blacksmith, yes
terday, alleging . failure to provide. The
Aldens were married in 1886 in San
Joaquin County.
To Seellger's diplomacy is due the peace
ful ending of what promised to be a sen
sational suit. He agreed to give his wife
the alimony she demanded, but refused to,
give his consent to her taking the house
hold furniture, giving as his reason that
he expected his wife would return to lum
within a year. He offered her the value
of the furniture in order that their house
might not be disturbed. Her husband's
kindness caused Mrs. Seeliger to feel that
she was doing him an Injustice in, charg
ing him with cruelty, and she consented
to meet him. They met and a dismissal
of the divorce suit followed.
The suit for divorce instituted against
Ludwig Seeliger, the prominent butcher
at 3318 Mission street, . by Annie Seeliger
will not come, up for trial. The couple
have patched up their differences and will
resume the happy relations which existed
before Mrs. Seeliger sought the aid of the
divorce courts. She charged her husband
with treating her cruelly and asked the
court to awarfl her $75 alimony.

The disappearance of Thomas W.
Fisher, wholesale liquor merchant, was
reported to the police yesterday. He left
his home, 2906 Bush street, nine days ago
and has not been seen or heard of since.
He is a member of the firm of Fisher &
King. 12 Mission street, and a prominenf
member of the Odd Fellows. No reason
can be assigned for his disappearance.

"Congressional action has placed the
final determination of the right of admis
sion of Chinese in executive officers, with
out judicial intervention, andv this has
been for many years the* recognized and
declared policy of the country. The reg
ulations to prevent the abuse of the priv
ilege of transit have been and are Intend
ed to effectuate the same policy, and re
course to the courts by habeas â¢ corpus to
determine the existence of such abuses.
The record did not present a case of reg
ulation or of action in contravention with
the constitution, and we think that upon
the admission of fact the. orders of the
Collector cannot be held to have been in
valid." . - > â¢

SWINDLED CHINAMAN
DEPLORES * HIS LOSS
ELLIS BAKES A VICTIM
OF KNOCKOUT DROPS
That He Was Not Sand-
Police Have Come to the Conclusion
bagged.
Ellis Baker, the watchman of a chicken
car at Six"th and Townsend streets, who
was supposed to have been sandbagged
and robbed in the car early Sunday morn
ing, regained consciousness yesterday
morning at the Emergency Hospital. He
made a statement that he had been drink
ing with three strange men in a saloon
in the neighborhood and remembered re
turning to the car and entering it, but
that was all he could remember. He had
been robbed of about $20. He had him
self placed the dead chickens in the sacks
to throw them away, as they were use
less for food.
As there were no bruises .or cuts on any
portion of Bauer's body the police have
arrived at the conclusion that he was
given "knockout drops" in the saloon
and robbed after he had become uncon
scious in the car. Every effort will be
made to discover the men who adminis
tered the "knockout drops," but with
small chance of success, as Baker says
he cannot recall the faces of the three
men. He was removed from the hospital
to the New Western Hotel yesterday aft
ernoon.
Object to Use of Stock.
The Troy Steam Laundry Company is
suing for an injunction restraining Pum
yea Williamson, Howard Welch and W.
A. Asmussen from disposing of 1000 shares
of stock in the corporation and from
voting- the stock at the 'next meeting of
the - directors of the corporation. It is
charged that they obtained the stock in
order to secure the control of the board
of directors.
Jansen' s Cattle Seized.
United States Marshal John H. Shine
has levied an execution on a drove of cat
tle on the- ranch of John Jansen. near
Scotts Bar, Siskiyou County, to collect a
judgment for $2500 obtained by the United
States in a suit against Jansen for cut
ting Government timber.
The selections hold good for the entire
round trip. An invitation signed by W.
B. Dennett, secretary of the Fresno
Chamber of Commerce, was received yes
terday, in which the business men of San
Francisco were asked to be the guests of
the Fresno Chamber of Commerce. Mr.
Jennings accepted for the committee. The
party will be three hours in Fresno.
Among those to make the trip will be the
leading men in all the commercial organi
zations of San Francisco. The compli
ment accorded to the interior, is in the
same line as the action of the Promotion
Committee, which is working to promote
the prosperity of the counties north of
Tehachapl.
One hundred leading business houses of
San Francisco will be represented by the
party of business men who will start next
Thursday evening on a special train for a
tour of the San Joaquin Valley, making
Bakersfield the initial point of inspection.
This fact was given out by Rufus P. Jen
nings yesterday. The special train will
consist of five Pullmans. When the en
gineer throws open the throttle of the big
locomotive a pilgrimage of a sort that
is new to Sail Francisco will begin. No
one along the route will be in doubt what
the special train signifies. Banners with
letters large enough for reading at a dis
tance, as the train is whirled down the
valley and up again will make this an
nouncement: "San Francisco Business
Men's Excursion to Bakerstield and the
San Joaquin Valley." Â¦ .â¢ - .

for the trip are asked by the committee to

The rush to the Northwest is due to the moderate
price of land, and if price alone be considered we can
not compete,- but when the fertility of the soil and
the value of its products are taken into consideration
the Northwest cannot compete with California. It
ought to be possible therefore to bring the better and
wiser class of home-seekers to this State if for no
other purpose than that of taking a look at the land/
All of that is interesting to California. We have
ample lands for settlers"" and a far better market for
the sale of produce. Furthermore/we have an un
rivaled soil and climate for the production of about
everything' a farmer wishes to cultivate. We. ought
therefore to be receiving a' considerable part of the
stream of migration that has now' started from the
East. The task of attracting the attention . of home
seekers to Northern California could not have been
undertaken at a more opportune time.
As a result of the migration the railroads \ of the
Northwest are estimated to have carried 25,000 cars
of freight belonging to the home-seekers. It is added
by the correspondent of the Sun that all prospects
point to, the conclusion that the migration during
April and May will be nearly double that of the past
two months. Much of it .will go â¢ to Canada, and
Manitoba expects to add 30,000 people to her popula
tion during the summer..

The Dallas Morning News in its Issue of
Wednesday, April 23, showed an enterprise
and capacity equal to many of the great-
Metropolitan dailies. In size, matter and
Illustration, the paper reflected credit on
the publishers, and on the city which
boasts â¢ its presence and accords it sup
port. The occasion of the effort was the
opening day - of the Confederate veteran
'reunion held in Dallas during. the closlnjr
days of last month. . _.:...
The issue consisted of six sections of
twelve pages each, three, of which were
devoted to historical reminiscences, i n
prose and poetry, of the Civil war, battles
fought off Texas coast. Famous Texans
and bodies of Texans who served the lost
cause, etc., illustrated with pictures' of
honored Confederates still living, and
others with, honors unf aded .who marched
ahead "to "fame's eternal camDine
.ground." -,
, Pictures of beautiful girls, daughters of
old Confederates, sponsors for delegations
from the several States lend a winning at
traction to many of the pages.
: The last two sections are given' to set
ting forth the beauties and advantages of
Dallas,: and the claims of her business
men, who have-not been at all backward
in their demands for space.
If -the; report* be true that Roosevelt authorized
Poet Laureate Austin to dedicate that book of poems
to him, he can no longer be justly charged with be
ing too headstrong and strenuous. The mild Â¦ and
gentle patience displayed by such an acceptance at
tests a disposition ; to bear anything without kicking.

A DISPATCH from St. Paul to the New York
Sun says: "In the. sixty days ending with
April 12 the railroads brought 136,000 home
seekers from: the Eastern and Southern States to the
Northwest. Of these it is estimated that 50 per cent
have gone through the -city, 25 per cent have gone by
way, of the Burlington road, via Billings, and,.2S per
cent have either settled in or have passed through
South Dakota into North Dakota." ' â¢ â¢
MIGRATING HOME-SEEKERS.
A St. Louis Councilman who' is nestling quietly
in Mexico safe" from extradition has unknowingly
made glad the hearts of many Councilmen who may
or may not hpnor our great American municipalities.
He is charged with bribery, and our treaty with Mex
ico says that it is not one of the offenses for. which
a man may be extradited.
Major Strong, the abandoned son of a worthy
man, and the more abandoned woman who travels
with him have reached New York and the light of
new notoriety. It is unfortunate that the earth was
not large enough for a hole in which they could hide
from the sight of decent men.

At, the Stockton session E. I. Wolfe of
San Francisco, at present grand sub chief
ranger, will become the grand chief
ranger, succeeding John Heenan. H. A.
Gabriel of Santa Clara, the grand senior
woodward.' will become sub chief, and E.
B., Collins of Chico and H. L. Simon of
San Francisco are the most prominent,
candidates for. the office of senior wood
ward. John J. Cordy, the incumbent, will
be a candidate for re-election for grand
seoretary, with a possibility of opposition.
Frank Conlin will be re-elected grand
treasurer if he desires to serve again, and
J. E. O'Brien will be a candidate for
grand recording secretary. .
. During the session there will be pre
sented forty amendments to the laws, but
the majority are only slight changes to
The reports that are, to be presented at
the session of the Grand Court^of the For r
esters of America,. which opens in Stock
ton to-day, will show a membership In
this State of 17,900, and a gain of 2500 dur
ing the "year. This membership is distrib
uted, in 179 courts. The membership in the
United States Is In excess of 230,000. Since
its organization the order has distributed
in sick and funeral benefits and In dona
tions to members in distress $8,520,706, of
which. $1,020,700 was distributed during the
past year.

SOME OF THE OFFICERS OF THE
CALIFORNIA GRAND COURT,
FORESTERS OF AMERICA. .
Should it be foufid necessary for the powers to in
terfere a second time the consequences are likely/to
be serious not only for China, but for the whole*
world. Sharp disagreements arose between the offi
cers and the men of the various nations on the march
to Peking, and grave differences arose among diplo
mats and statesmen afterward. Those differences
have^been compromised, but they have not been set
tled; and now if a new policy is called for in dealing
with Ctina there may be war* in earnest
The critical nature of the situation is disclosed by
a dispatch from Washington to the effect that the
diplomats in that -city regard it as extremely probable
a general uprising throughout the empire .will oc
cur, and should that be so, the powers would have
to adopt toward China a policy widely different from
that pursued in suppressing the Boxer outbreak. It
seems that the "salutary lesson" which the powers
thought they were administering by the invasion has
proven to be anything but salutary. Nor can the re
sult be wondered at The harshness with which the
country was treated during the march to Peking was
not likely to diminish the hatred of the natives for
foreigners, and does not appear to hive caused so
% much wholesome terror of European I arms as was
hoped for. The consequence is that having done the
business wrong in the first instance, the powers may
have to undertake it again and do it differently.
From those facts we may conclude that while the
movement is liberal in its origin and leadership, the
disturbances of the country have had the inevitable
effect of arousing the worst elements of the popula
tion, and the leaders have been unable to control
them. The insurrection is no longer guided by an
intelligent spirit directed, to a definite end. It has
become something like the rising of a mob and car
ries destruction to everything in its way to which any
considerable element of the mob objects, whether
it be foreign or domestic.
While it is said by persons who are familiar with
Chinese affairs that Sun Yat Sen has the sympathy
of thousands of wealthy and progressive Chinese who
are opposed to the Dowager Empress and are eager
to bring China into harmony with Western civiliza
tion, the progress of the insurrection has been
marked by deeds which clearly attest that all of his
followers are not of that mind. Several missionaries
have been driven from their stations, the Christian
converts have been dispersed, one Jesuit has been
murdered, and there have been .observed among the
rioters persons carrying banners inscribed with the
words "Abolish the missionaries !"
That the progressive class of Chinese are active in
the movement is made certain by the fact that one of
its most conspicuous leaders is Sun Yat Sen, who is
well known as an opponent of the present dynasty,
and whose long residence in the United States and
Europe has made him a convert to Western ideas.
Some j-ears ago Sun Yat Sen was the subject of a
sensational incident in diplomacy. After flying from
.China to escape the Dowager Empress he went to
London, and while there was kidnaped on the street
and was taken to the Chinese embassy, where he
was held as a prisoner. Only the most strenuous
protests from the British Government saved him
from being put to death there and then. Upon being
released Sun Yat kept himself hidden for some time,
but he appears to have found encouragement enough
to induce him to return to China, where he has since
taken a prominent part in fomenting the insurrection.
REPORTS concerning the disturbances in
China are conflicting as regards the motives
and objects of the leaders of the insurrection,
but all agree that the situation is critical. It is said
by some that the disorders have been occasioned by
progressive Chinamen, who desire to overthrow the
Manchu dynasty and set up a more liberal govern
ment. Others assert that the disturbances are a re
vival of the old Boxer outbreak against foreigners,
and represent anything rather than liberalism. Each
of these views probably contains an element of truth;
and the insurrection may prove to be an uprising of
all the discontented elements of the southern prov
inces of the empire, each hoping to profit by the dis
turbances that all help to create.
THE CHINESE PROBLEM.
It is. to be borne in mind, of course, that the
Hampton school is designed not to make scholars,
but workers. As was stated by General Armstrong,
who founded the institute: '?The temporal salvation
of the colored race for some time to come is to be
Won out of the ground.- Skillful agriculturists' and
mechanics are needed rather than poets and orators."
It may be that the object for which they were work
ing had something to do in shaping the judgment of
the Hampton faculty on the comparative merits of
the two systems of training. What has been found
helpful to -the and aimed at by 'Hampton might not
be found helpful in a school with different objects in
view. Under any circumstances, however, the re
port is interesting, and if may be this colored school
is working out a system of education which the
whites may find it of advantage Â£0 adopt, at least to
a partial extent. -
While it is not safe to argue from this report from
Hampton that it would be better to increase the
amount of industrial training in .our .public schools
by making a corresponding diminution in the
amount of academic training, still .such argument as
can be drawn from itjat all, is. decidedly in. favor. of
industrial education. The boy who learns to do
things develops intellectually up to a. certain point
as much. as the boy who learns to know things, and
has the advantage that his ability to do something
that the world is willing to pay for gives him a cer
tain independence which the other does not possess,
and from that sense of usefulness comes a moral as
well as a mental gain.
"Concerning the experiment the report says: "We
have watched with interest the results of these two
kinds of training and, while it is yet too early to
speak definitely, we incline to the opinion that our
trade school boys, who set out in their second year
to master some particular branch of industry, are
stronger, mentally and morally, than those who take
the regular academic course. It may be said that the
object of. school life is' not to make carpenters, but
to make men. Whi!e this is true, the making of a
good carpenter goes far toward the making of a
good man. The effect, upon these young people of
feeling that they are able to produce something valu
able, for 'which the world is willing to pay, is most
helpful."
â¢The report ' sets forth some of the results attained
by industrial education as compared with academic
education. A large .number of boys, after speeding
a year at farm work, working- air day and attending
the night school, proceed to take three years in the
trade school, continuing to attend the night school
for academic training,' while others spend the three
years in the academic department and take manual
training twice -a week. Thus jhere is a fair chance to
note which training is .best. . . -
It, is not upon ;numbers only, however, that the
reputation of; the institute rests. 'While it does not
aim at what is generally known as "higher educa
tion," it none the less accomplishes a widely useful
work in fitting the youth of the negro race for living
useful and successful lives' and in training teachers
who are to. help forward the great work. The re
sults of ; the experiments of such a school may be
studied with profit by all who are interested in educa
tional methods. ' \' - â¢' c ;;Â¦;,
HAMPTON INSTITUTE has grown to be one
of r our most notable educational centers. Ac
cording to the 'thirty-fourth annual .report,
which has just been published, 1079 youths of both
sexes have received . instruction on the. school
grounds during the winter term, the attendance at the
last summer institute was 347, and the industrial
classes at Norfolk have an enrollment of 1976, while
the Huntington classes, at /Newport News have an
attendance of 807, making â¢ a 1 total' of 4209 students at
Hampton, "or. in its immediate neighborhood, under
the care of the institute. So far as the number of
students goes, therefore, it is one of the. foremost
schools of the country. . >
INDUSTRIAL TRAINING.
Another of our: idols has" been smashed and the
busy iconoclast: in Congress has. armed his leveling
blows at one of the dearest to us. * The chairman of
the House Naval Committe^e says our navy isn't
worth shucks in actual warfare, and yet we are afraid
to ask him what he thinks of the battles of Manila
Bay and Santiago. . , - '
murder of women, and the danger line will be passed
in safety. If this is not done, the breach made by
Sibley is sure to tfiden and thousands will- walk
through it. Wise men have not failed to note the
utter aSsence of popular enthusiasm about the war.
One Major General, in his testimony before the Phil
ippine Committee of the Senate, said that it was a
conquest that had no glory, and that he had winced at
every blow he had been compelled to strike at a de
fenseless people. All such expressions combine to
attAct on one side the public sentiment that is re
pelled on the other by deeds that make an American
toss on his pillow and dream uneasily. Let leaders
be admonished, and beware the danger line.
Let the members and leaders of the Republican
party not only heed but lead this sentiment. Let them
declare that the party is not to be identified with the
wholesale murder of children and worse than the
From a merely partisan point of view this is most
fortunate for the Republican party. There was a
manifest determination in some quarters to suppress
all criticism of such acts of savage brutality as have
disgraced our arms in Samar and elsewhere. With a
well-defined insolence the people affected with Fun
stonism had gone forth snubbing as "sickly senti
mentality" every expression of humanity which dep
recated the universal arson and slaughter of children,
to make a howling wilderness of the country. There
was a feeling that if such appalling deeds were the
price .of conquest it was too much .to pay. But cer
tain smug clerics and smugger laymen were deriding
this rising spirit Sibley has given.it liberty to speak,
and the- conscience of the country is enfranchised to
be uttered once more.
From another quarter rises a sentiment which will
ally itself to disappointed commercial expectation.
This is the growing bitterness against military meth
ods. When- this was confined to the anti-imperialists
it caused no uneasiness. They were led by men of
impracticable views and methods, who had proceeded
on lines that did not command the support of num
bers whose general sympathies were that way. But
now this is all changed. The speech of Sibley of
Pennsylvania, a Republican and a leading"expansion
ist, has given voice to long-suppressed feeling. His
speech was immediately followed by a release of Re
publican sentiment most formidable in its character.
IT is evident that our military administration in
the Philippines is approaching the danger line.
" By that we mean the line beyond which public
sentiment becomes a hostile force. The risk may not
be limited to the military policy and practice alone,
but arises also- in features of our civil policy.--.;
It is a strange and inconsistent feature in the" latter
that all the voices which from the beginning have
been raised for the permanent/ occupancy of the Phil
ippines have planted that policy upon the argument
of cqmmcrcial profit. By commerce in the island
products we are to get back the cost of war, we art
to pay ihe. military pensions, a bountiful crop of
which our conquest daily sows the seed, and the
country has been taught to look over the smoke of
battle, far beyond its bloody fields, to the commer
cial future. This enchanting picture of prpfit has
been spread in all its colors before the American vis
ion, and the effect has been to raise expectation and
to whet appetite and reconcile vast numbers to the
cost of securing results so satisfactory to the com
mercial spirit.â.
But when this method of pacifying our own people
has worked its way, the laws of Congress come, pro
hibiting the entry of American capital and energy
into the islands in the only form in which a dollar
of profit can be gained. The effect of our
is to exclude from the whole archipelago everything
American except the army and a few civuV office
holders and some school teachers. Economic effort
for whatever it may be worth as a placating force
is absolutely excluded. *ThÂ£ Call has printed from the
letters of Frank Carpenter and the speeches of Sen
ator Beveridge, Mr. Barrett, and others ' the com.
mercial view, directing the only way in which ex
pectations of profit can be realized, and we have also
tried to demonstrate that commercial exploitation
may mean material progress of the natives, and cause
them to look more kindly upon our presence there.
But it is not the policy of Congress, so that the sen
timent which that expectation has controlled may
Jose its interest in the Philippines. '

Hatters of Interest to Mariners and
NEWS OF THE OCEAN".
U. S. REVENUE CUTTER
WHICH WILL SAIL FOR
ALASKA THIS MONTH.
The speaking of ihe Red Rock has
started afresh the talk of a job on the
part of unknown speculators. The Red
Rock left Fraser River December 3 for
Queenstown". with a cargo of salmon. She
sailed in the gale that sent to the bottom
the British gunboat Condor and wrecked
the collier Matteawan. A few weeks
later a life buoy, bearing her name was
picked up. Then there came ashore sev
eral cases, of salmon said to have been
stowed in the limejuicer's hold. News of
these discoveries was promptly cabled to
London, where the underwriters in their
eagerness to get from under sent up the
rate of reinsurance to 50 per cent. The
eagerness with which the risk was played
at this figure made the British market
suspicious, and the Red Rock rate fell to
10 per cent. About the time the ship was
due at Queenstown a few more cases of
salmon were found on the northern beach.
"With the report of this latest find was
cabled the Information that the washed
up salmon had been ."stowed in the ves
sel's lower hold. It waf this last "dis
covery" and the care with which the re
port of It was sent abroad that gave rise
to the suspicion that the. northern beach
might have been salted at the instigation
of unscrupulous speculators. The exist
ence of this suspicion frustrated the Job,
If job it was, and although the. rate has
climbed up to 30 per cent that latest dis
covery of salmon had little effect on the
market. The losing overboard of cases of
salmon is an unusual occurrence. It hap
pened two years ago on the British ship
Ardnamurchan. She was placed on the
reinsured list, and those that played her
to arrive won handsomely. It is thought
likely that the case of tne Ardnamurchan
gave the clew for the manipulation of the
Red Rock scheme. The arrival of the
Red Rock at Queenstown is awaited with
interest, and if the captain's report fails
to confirm the loss of salmon there will
be a rigid .investigation into the source of
the reports ,that caused the underwriters
to reinsure their risk.
started,' was spoken April 11 in
latitude -1 north, longitude 30 west. Word
of the speaking was cabled yesterday ,to
the Merchants' Exchange, and the Red
Rock, quoted at 30 per cent, was wiped
off the reinsurance board. The Red Rock
was spoken about 350 miles northeast of.
Cape St. Roque,' and is due any day how
at Queenstown. . .

II and which has been on the rein-

weema, for San Pedro. f

Shipping Intelligence.
The carrying away of a davit on the steam
schooner Marshfleld yesterday afternoon pre
cipitated Michael Cullen, a sailor, to the deck.
He fell head down and sustained fractures of
the skull which will prove fatal.
Sustains Fatal Hurt.
Four stowaways and three passengers, all
Japanese, who arrived here on the Nippon
Maru.j were yesterday ordered deported. The
stowaways were denied landing on the ground
that they might become public charges; the
passengers were ordered back to Japan on the
showing of the Federal quarantine officials that
they were suffering from trachoma, a disease
of the eyes, contagious and not wanted in
America.
Denied Landing.
John J.' Searey, secretary of the Marine En
gineers' Association, has been dangerously ill
from blood-poisoning for the last two weeks,
and until yesterday Drs. Leland and Taylor be
lieved that the amputation of his left leg
would be necessary to save his. life. Saturday,
however. Searey took a turn for the better.
Yesterday the doctors informed the association
that they thought they could pull him through
without resort to heroic measures. It will be
some weeks before Searey is able to resume
his duties, which are now being conducted by
Acting Secretary J. M. Benson.
Searey Convalescent.
was Inspected' by Major Devol and Superin
tendent Pierce. She will sail for Manila May
15. The Grant, which has been the most serv
iceable of all the transports, will be overhauled
before she goes back to Manila. ,

Will Inspect Canneries.
The Cnited States Revenue cutter Perry
came alongside Filbert-street wharf yesterday
to coal and to undergo general repairs. She
trill be ready for her annual cruise about the
end of the month. The particular duty for
which she has been detailed is the inspection
of the different Alaskan cannery stations. The
revenue cutter McCulloch anchored yesterday
In man-of-war row. The McCulloch Is the
most graceful Government veesel that comes
into this harbor, and she never looked better
than she does to-day.

Falls Down Hold. j
Carberry Egan, a longshoreman, fell down
the hold of the British ship Lydgate yesterday,
lacerated his scalp, injured his hips and frac-
tured his shoulder blade.

Office of U. S. Light-House Inspector, Portland
Oregon. May 2, 1002.
Captain J. Christiansen, of the schooner
Â¦Winchester, reports to the Branch Hydro-
graphic Office that on March 28, 1002. at 6 45
p. m.. while lying becalmed in latitude N 12
degrees 40 minutes, longitude W. 166 degrees
with overcast sky and an occasional rain pass-
ing over from E. S. E.. he observed the sky to
light up suddenly, for a height of about 10
degrees and length of about 30 degrees of the
horizon with a bright red glow, to the north-
weet of his position, and at a distance which
he judged to be about twenty miles. This con-
tinued for about ten minutes, when it was ob-
scured by a rain cloud. For another ten min-
utes he saw a bright .white light, something
Â¦-M 1 , lar - t0 K h f , Welsbac h lighten color and
â¢ Ser> and on accent of the
rain In .<Hstlnet. The captain tried to make way
toward the light, but was prevented by lack of
JTV"?;* i H t kep V a brIght Uebt burning and a
bright, lookout, but saw no more of it after the
moon rose, a short time later.
_ , W. H. STANDLEY,
; Lieutenant, U. S. N. f in charge.

Provisions.
NEW YORK, May 5. â The disturbance and
anxiety which were felt in the financial world
this morning were much relieved before the
close of the day on the Stock Exchange. It
was known before the stock market opened
that the hopes entertained on Saturday of se
curing means to tide over the firms threatened
with embarrassment had failed, and there was
nothing to do for these houses but to announce
their inability to meet their contracts. In two
cases the falluree were fully expected, but the
third came as something of a surprise. There
was heavy liquidation on .the Stock, Exchange
when they were announced, but the effective
support offered the general market checked
the indiscriminate liquidation and Induced more
sober reflections. There is no doubt much dis
trust was felt for the fabric of credits gener
ally, owing to the ignorance of the extent to
which the collapse of the Webb-Meyer clocks
bad involved others. The development in the
stocks themselves was recognized to be not of
first Importance. The collapse of their prices
naturally reduced the' basis of credits, which
had been acquired on the fictitious and manip
ulative valuation. But the dazzling rise in the
prices for these securities had not attracted
any great speculative following. The trouble was
thus localized within a comparatively email
circle of Immediate participants in the specu
lation. This was soon demonstrated by the
movement of the general market and the ab
sence of financial trouble in other quarters.
There was the fear this morning that the re
eults must be faced of similar operations ;of
other pools in various specialties and even 'of
manipulative advances in standard railway
stocks; but the readiness with which offerings
were absorbed quieted the nervous excitement
and Induced some buying back of stocks by
hasty sellers. In fact, the day's net losses are
found to be quite Insignificant as a rule, and
not a few important etocks closed higher than
Saturday. The reports of copious rains In the
Southwest and the effective breaking of the
drouth conditions, which have kept awake anx
iety regarding the crops, was a material aid to
the recovery. The persistent upward course of
Louisville and Nashville was a sustaining in
fluence. Rumors were renewed of a combina
tion of this read with the â¢ Chicago, Indiana
polis and Louisville. The prospect of a strike
on the part of the anthracite miners and a
stringency of the money market will amply ac- .
count for a repre^Jon of speculation. As a
matter of fact, the active liquidation of the
first hour accounted for the early one-half of
the day's total dealings. Money loaned as
high as 20 per cent, and receded to 6 per cent
only after the principal demands had been met
There can be no doubt the discrimination
against collateral and unfavorable disposi
tion developed among money lenders toward the
recent indiscriminate tendency of the specu
lation were factors In the money stringency
The squeezing out process along this line is
viewed with satisfaction. The knowledge that
credits are very liberally expanded at present
and that the rather meager cash reserves are
subject to heavy demands has caused real anx
iety to be felt from the reckless speculation
indulged tn In some quarters.
Some of the speculative bonds were weak un
der forced liquidation, but the market recov
ered and became irregular in sympathy with
stocks. Total ealee. $4,030,000.
United States bonds were all unchanged on
thÂ£_last call.

San Francisco and vicinity â Cloudy Tuesday,
with occasional showers; fresh southwest
winds. ALEXANDER G. McADIE,
Forecast Official.
Southern California â Cloudy, unsettled
weather Tuesday, possibly light showers; light
eouthwest winds.
Nevada â Cloudy, wanner Tuesday: fresh
southerly winds.
The temperature has risen over the northern
half of the coast and has remained nearly sta
tionary elsewhere. Maximum temperatures in
the San Joaquln Valley and the southern part
of the State range from 76 to 84 degrees.
Light showers have fallen in Northern Cali
fornia.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. May 6, 1902:
Northern California â Cloudy Tuesday, with
occasional light showers; fresh southerly winds.
The pressure has fallen slowly over the coast
from Eureka northward. A slight disturbance
is passing over Oregon and conditions are fa
vorable for showery weather on the coast north
of San Francisco.
FORECAST.
WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERALr
.CHICAGO, May 5. â Bears started In to dom
inate- wheat at the sound of the opening gong.
Bearish news was at hand in good array.
Cables were lower, receipts were improving,
but greatest of all factors was the heavy
rains that had come to the relief of Kansas and
Nebraska. Private wires had reports of crop
damages as usual, but the trade came to the
speedy conviction that all was not lost In the
Southwest. Estimates of the Kansas wheat
yield that had been as low as 25,000,000 bushels
a week ago were raised to-day by conservative
people to 50,000,000 bushels. St. Louis and
Kansas City sold heavily. July wheat opened
%<S%c to %@%c lower at 75%@75%c, touched
75%c and then slumped to 74%@~4%c. There
was a large short interest In wheat and on the
decline offerings were absorbed. Prices for
July rallied to 75c. .but at the close were weak,
l%c lower at 74%@75c. : .
Corn ruled weak on the Influence of rains
and the depression in wheat. July closed 94@
%c lower at 00%@i50%c.
Rains had a weakening effect in oats and
under the influence of the breaks in other
grains oats declined steadily. July closed %c
Icwer at 35%C
Steady hog prices at the yards held prices In
provisions from slumping in the pit". The close
was about steady, July pork a shade lower and
lard and ribs 2%a down. ;
The leading futures ranged as follows:

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Flurry in a minor group of Wall-street stocks.
Local Stocks and Bonds quiet and featureless.
Silver and Exchange as before quoted.
Wheat futures lower and Barley futures higher.
Oats firm, with minors of export orders.
Corn strongly held at previous prices. Rye still dull. Â¦
Bran advanced 50c. Middlings and Hay unchanged.
Continued shipping demand for Beans.
Butter steady: Cheese weak and Eggs firm. Â¦
Some contracts for new drkd Apricots reported.
Expected advance in Cured Meats not yet realized.
New York looking for a squeeze in Lard.
Hops quiet, with fine crop prospects.
Mutton in better supply and easier. Other Meats unchanged.
Potatoes and Onions firmly held. Vegetables dull.
Two cars of Eastern poultry marketed.
Oranges and Lemons still dull. Bananas firm.

st.. 20 horses, all styles cjmp. peddling and

ness and rubber tire buggies.Will Now Control Lighting of
Two Counties.
United Gas and Electric Company
A deal was closed yesterday by which
the United Gas and Electric Company
acquired the interests of the Consolidated
Light and Poer Company, the San Jose
Light and Power Company and the Elec
tric Improvement Company of San Fran
cisco and San Jose, the price being placed
at $1,500,000.
The purchasers by this deal have gained
complete control of all lighting and power
business in San Mateo and Santa Clara
counties. Â¦
The new owners will make many im
provements, among which will be the lay
ing of a main for distribution of gas be
tween San Jose and San Mateo. All the
electric power will be supplied by the
Standard Electric Company.
J. E. Green, president of the United Gas
arid Electric Company, and C. â¢ W. Mc-
Afee, president of the Electric Improve
ment Company, have confirmed the story
of the sale. The plans of improvements
have been figured out and the necessary
work wlil begin soon.
Smashed Valuable Windows.
J. M. Wood, a salesman, while under
the influence of liquor early yesterday
morning, threw a brick through the plate
glass window in Nolan's shoe store, 10)24
Market street, and another through the
window of the Columbia Suit Company's
store next door. He was arrested by Po
liceman Fraher on a charge of burglary,
but when the case was called In ; Judge
Cabaniss' court yesterday the charge was
changed to malicious mischief ! and the
case continued till this morning. . . " â¢ â¢'
Zaragosa Club Celebrates.
Mayor E. E.' Schmitz was the .central
figure at the celebration of the Zaragosa
Club held last night at Washington
square Hall in honor of the victory o^
that doughty general at Puebla on May
5 1862. He was introduced by Judge A. B.
Treadwell, and related during his dis
course many historical episodes.;' An in
teresting programme of musical and liter
ary exercises followed, dancing conclud
ing the evening's pleasure. . .
STOCKTON, May ' 5.â Rufe \ Turner
knocked out Tim Hegarty, of Australia in
the last of the seventh o-ound to-night in
a hurricane fight in which the loser had
none the worst of it until he was put to
sleep He was the aggressor from start
to finish and was not afraid to mix it at
any time. He showed that he has a hard
punch and is a great ring general. After.
Hegarty had sent in a half-dozen blow* in
the seventh and was forcing the fighting,
Turner caught him with a left jolt on the
stomach and a right cross to the jaw,
which made it "curtains." â¢ /

HBALDSBURG, May 5. â Francis
George Smith committed' suicide last
night In a cell in the City Jail, during a
fit Of Insanity. Smith, who was 51 years
of age, was a married man, a native of
Germany; and owned a goat ranch in the
mountains twenty-nine miles west of
Healdsburg, where he lived with his wife.
Mrs. Smith came to town on Saturday
and got a warrant for the arrest of her
husband oh a charge of insanity. She then
returned 'home and told her husband that
officers were coming after him. Deputy
Sheriff J. C. Ingalls went after Smith
early yesterday morning. He met Mrs.
Smith on the road several mile3 from her
home. Leaving her there, he proceeded to
the house, and when within 100 yards of
it heard the report of a gun. The officer
hurried into> the house and found one
room full of powder smoke, but Smith
had lied. Following fresh tracks in the
grass which led into a deep canyon a half
mile away, he discovered Smith behind
some brush,, a large bjitcher knife* In his
hand and the blood running from his neck
and left wrist. The officer drew his pistol,
which was not loaded, and ordered Smith
to drop his knife. Smith begged him not
to shoot and readily surrendered.
Ingalls brought the insane man to town,
placing him in a cell in the City Jail.
When the cell was entered this morning
it was found that Smith had made
another : and this time a successful at
tempt to shuffle off this mortar coll. The
iron - bedstead in the cell is fastened to
the wall on either end by a slack chain.
Smith used the slack in one of these
chains to make a half hitch around his
neck, and then dropping on his knees suc
ceeded In strangling himself.
gles Himself With Chain
From a Bedstead.

H. J. Edwards, the present general
manager of the Electrical Improvement
Company, is slated for local manager of
the new corporation.
The new owners plan to combine the
various electric and gas plants In this
city into one huge affair. In addition they
will erect a ?2o0,000 'â¢ electric distributing
station in this city, as well: as lay a largo
gas main from. San Jose up the west side
of the bay to supply all the towns af
fected bv the deal. In short, the operat
ing headquarters of the huge enterprise
is to be located here.
SAN JOSE, May 5.â Upon the authority
of the executive officers of -the interested
corporations it is announced that both
the San Jose Light and Power Company
and the Electric Improvement Company,
the local lighting enterprises, have passed
into the control of the United Qas and
Electric Company of San Francisco.' The
consideration involved is $1,250,000, of
which $700,000 goes to the Improvement
Company. The deal is on a cash basis,
the new owners paying cash for all the
offered stock of each corporation. In the
merger is included the Consolidated Light
and Power- Company, the present owners
of the lighting interests in all the towns
in Han Mateo County. The Palo Alto,
Menlo Park and Mountain View plants
Â£irfe Included under separate purchase.

The annual meeting of the San Fran
cisco section of the Council of Jewish
Women was held yesterday afternoon in
Temple Emanu-El. The officers of the
council presented their reports, showing
the present condition of the society and
also giving a resume of the work accom
plished during the past year. A com
munication from the peace committee at
Chicago, which is the headquarters of the
council, was received, requesting the local
society to observe the 15th of May.
The officers' reports showed that the
council is in a most flourishing condition.
A very interesting report was presented
by Miss Jessica Peixotto, president of the
Philanthropic Circle, describing the In
vestigations of economic problems which
had been pursued by the circle during the
year. Reports were also read by Mrs. M.
C. Sloss, president of the council; Miss
Mabel Baum, secretary; Miss Mamie
Voorsanger, corresponding secretary and
treasurer pro tem., and' Mrs. Andrew-
Davis of the Bible and ethical depart
ments.

Flames Cut Them Off From Avenues
of Escape and They Are
Suffocated.
NEW YORK, May 5.â Two persons per
ished and several were injured to-day in
a fire that gutted the building at 304 Pearl
street, occupied by the Eureka Bedding
Company. The dead: *
ANNA BOL.TCHER, 19 years of age.
f JOHN LYNCH.
The injured: Ethel McGrath; burned,
probably fatally; Katie Wadsworth, con
tusions of back and internal injuries, and
Annie Gilroy, sprains and bruises.
The fire started on the first floor near a
pile of stair pads and loose cotton, ap
parently from a lighted cigar or cigarette
stub. The cotton burst into a blaze, the
stair pads were set fire and in an instant
the flames spread throughout the first
floor and mounted upward.
A panic ensued and disregarding the
fire escapes many of the employes jumped
! from rear windows to the roof of an ex
tension. It was in this way that Miss
.Gilroy and Miss Wadsworth were hurt.
The bodies of Miss Boltcher and Lynch
were found on the top floor, death having
resulted from burns and suffocation.

IN A NEW YORK FIRE
WASHINGTON, May 5.â A large dele
gation appeared before a sub-committee
of the House Committee on Foreign Af
fairs in the interest of the Boers, Dr.vJ.
C. Hexamer of the National German Al
liance speaking first. He described the
heroic struggle of the Boers and urged
the adoption of a resolution asking the
President to communicate with Great Brit
ain an expression of sympathy for the
Boers and expressing the hope that tha
unfortunate differences may be settled.
He said that England at this time would
not take umbrage, being enfeebled with
the war. He said he advocated the reso
lutions as a Republican, and if they were
pigeonholed it would mean the loss of a
million votes for the German Alliance.
Dr. Keyser of the Boer legislative com
mittee said that the Governors of several
States were strongly pro-Boer. Through
Representative W. A. Smith a report of
I. M. de Bruyn was presented on the use
of New Orleans as a base for military
supplies for Great Britain. Others spoke
of the use of American ports as supply
camps for the British army. Representa
tive Smith also urged action by Congress.
Representative ' Sulzer said millions Â£OT
Americans would approve a resolution of
sympathy.
Representative Adams of the sub-com
mittee, closing the hearing, took excep
tion to the introduction of politics, say
ing no such question was involved. He
said it was not a matter of domestic poli
tics, but international law'.

the museum' additions, the university will
be finished In accordance. with Mrs. Stan
ford's wishes and plans.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, May 5.â
The latest announcement in building im
provements for Stanford is that plans
have been drawn by Resident Architect
C. E. Hodges for immense additions to
the university museum. Eight new build
ings are to be added in rectangular shape
at the rear of the present immense struc
ture, in such a manner as to inclose a
large court. This improvement will be
one of the largest and most important in
stituted on the campus this year. Con
tractors are now figuring on the cost of
the buildings and work upon them will
be begun during the summer months.

WICKLOW BOOTH GIVES
A NIGHT AT IRISH FAIR
PASADENA. May 5.â A telegram re
ceived to-day suddenly transformed
George A. Dold of this city from a day
laborer into thrice a millionaire. Dold is
a Virginian of English descent, a wan
derer on the face of the globe, but ne
comes of a wealthy family, and for twen
ty years he has been expecting this sum
mons from poverty to riches.
When he got the telegram he was hard
at work carting oranges for the Pasadena
Orange Growers* Association, at $1 50 a
day. He immediately quit work and de
manded his pay. Then he went to the
nearest clothing store and purchased a
suit.
"No more hard work for me," said he.
The estate of which Dold is one of the
heirs is valued at $15,000,000. It comes
from a line of Englishmen, the original
having been Captain Willis of tha French
army, a great-great-great-grandfather of
Dold on his mother's side. The estate
had been entangled In litigation for tha
past score of years, during which time
many heirs had died, leaving as the sola
inheritors on this side of the Atlantic
George A. Dold; his sister, Mrs. Fannie
Sands of Richmond, Va.; another sister,
Mrs. Willis Dold of Richmond; a brother,
Edwin P. Dold of Philadelphia, and a
cousin, Mrs. Thomas S. Dornnln of Nor
folk, Va. Major Sands, Dold's brother-in
law, who Is a lawyer* of Richmond, has
been working on the estate for years and
has won his case. There are several Eng
lish heirs, but It Is said that the $15,000,000
will be divided equally among the nv*
American heirs.
When Dold was asked to-day what he
intended doing with his vast wealth he
laughed and said he guessed he would get
married. He Is 40 years of age and his
parents are dead. His father and mother
owned a fine estate in Richmond at the
time of the war. Dold was for a time a
sailor. He enlisted with the Fourth Uni
ted States Cavalry, troop L, from San
Francisco and went to the Philippines,
where he served fifteen months. He ex
pects to get his money Immediately and
will settle in this city, after visiting rela
tives in the East.
