The executive committee of the Califor
nia Real Estate Association, together
with representatives of the co-operating
district associations of real estate men,
held an all-day session in the rooms of the
State Board of .Trade yesterday. C. M.
Wooster, president of the, new organiza
tion, occupied the chair. Vice President
David Bush and all the recently elected
officers were present. N. Â¦ P. Chipman,
president of the State Board of Trade, was
in attendance to advise with the real
estate executive committee." . Â£
The organization grew out of the present
movement to promote the interior counties
and those of the coast and there is sym
pathetic action with the State Board of
Trade. The business of the day largely
consisted of the adoption of a constitu
tion and by-laws. The provisions were in
substantial accord with the suggestions
that proved to be acceptable when the
initial meeting was held in the hall of the
Chamber of Commerce. On no point was
One ofYhe provis'ions of general interest
makes it necessary for any real estate
man 'to secure membership in the local
association situated nearest to his place of
residence as preliminary to the enjoyment
of the privileges of the State organiza
tion. By this plariv only those who make
dealing in real estate their principal busi
ness will be enabled to take share in the
work in view by the California associa
tion. - During the day the by-laws of or
ganization of rear estate d ealera *" ,^e
large cities of the East were consulted
â¢and the essential features of the Eastern
plans were adapted to local use after
thorough discussion. The action of yes
terday completes the organization of the
new body and puts it in a position to at
once engage in its chosen field.
Please hand this key to my brother, Joseph
Jacobs, room 14, Thurlow Block, 126 Kearny
street. I have nothing to say for publication
except that reverses caused by sickness is my
only reason. I had no trouble with any of my
relatives or outsiders. . :
At 5:30 o'clock on Sunday afternoon he
engaged a room at the Burlington and
mailed a letter to his brother Joseph, in
forming him that he had gone to the Bur
lington for the purpose of committing
suicide and that his body would be found
in his room in the morning. When Jo
seph Jacobs opened his mail yesterday
morning he was horrified on reading his
brother Samuel's letter. He hurried to
the Burlington, and in company with the
clerk entered the room and found the
body of the suicide lying on the bed with
a roll of cotton batting covering his
mouth and nostrils and tied in place with
a linen handkerchief. The batting had
been saturated with chloroform. On the
bureau were a bottle one-quarter full of
chloroform, a kev and a business card,
on the back of which the suicide had
written in pencil:
Samuel Jacobs, . - brother - of Assistant
United States Treasurer Julius Jacobs of
this city, committed suicide last Sunday
night in his room in the Burlington Lodg
ing-house on Market street by inhaling
chloroform. Continued 111 health and con
sequent reverses in business prompted the
rash deed. Samuel had been a partner
with his brother, Joseph Jacobs, in the
business of manufaturers' agents on
Kearny street, but retired from the firm
shortly before he left for Manila. His
health was wrecked in that tropical coun
try and he was obliged to return to this
city. He obtained employment as a book
keeper, but after a half day's attempt
he found that he was not strong enough
to Jift heavy account books and he re
signed. :.. . -
After Judge Troutt announced his de
cision that the court was not prejudiced
and therefore no transfer would U3 grant
ed Attorney â¢ Pence ; moved the court to
postpone the hearing of the petition for
final distribution on the ground that he
intended, to file a suit against some of the
property of the estate.
This announcement caused considerable
surprise and developed, quite an argu
ment. Mr. Wheeler arose after Mr.
Pence took his seat and moved the court
to dismiss Mrs. Craven as a litigant on
the ground that it had already been de
cided that she had no right to the Fair
millions or any portion of them, and on
the further ground that Judge Slack had
already decided that the deeds that Mrs.
Craven held to -the Pine and Sansome
street property and the property at
Twelfth and Mission streets were for
geries. It is over this property that Mrs.
Craven's attorneys intend to bring suit
again. The case then went over until this
morning at 11 o'clock.
Superior Judge Troutt yesterday denied
the motion of the attorneys for Mrs. Net
tie Craven to have the proceedings of
final distribution, and settlement of the
litigation over the estate of James G. Fair
transferred to another department of the
Superior Court. The transfer was re
quested on the ground that Judge Troutt
was biased, and prejudiced against Mrs.
Craven. Affidavits made by Mr. Pence
and Judge Maguire tending io show preju
dice and bias on the part of Judge Troutt
were introduced, and on the other hand
counter affidavits were introduced by the
attorneys of the Fair children tending to
prove the contrary. Considerable argu
ment was indulged in between Pence and
Maguire, representing Mrs. Craven, and
McEnerney and Wheeler for the Fair
children. . ' .
The sand teamsters, who threatened to
go on strike if their demand for an in
crease of wages was not granted, re
ported for duty yesterday to a man. This
action was the result of. an agreement
reached with the contractors by which
the wages of the teamsters will be raised
25 cents a day on the first Monday in Au
gust. The men were in good spirits, and
they tay that the other demands made by
them eventually will be granted. They
are satisfied with the victory gained thus
far.
Fifty suspended members were rein
stated and fifteen new members were ini
tiated. The affairs of the union are in ex
cellent shape.
Boxmakers' Union, No. 152. met last
night and indorsed a new scale of wages
to be presented to the employers next
week. The demands of the union were
not divulged, but it is understood a small
increase in wages and a nine-hour day
will be insisted upon. Mayor Schmitz ad
dressed the union and was the recipient
of considerable attention. His remarks
concerning the manner of conducting
strikes peaceably were greeted with ap
plause.
The fact that the Morton people still
employ non-union drivers is responsible
for the trouble. ' The employes of the Mor
ton company declined to join, the union,
claiming they were being paid better
wages than the union drivers. This con
tention is denied by the union men. who
are in consequence greatly angered at the
Morton drivers. For some time past a
bittc-r feeling has been developing and the
union men now threaten to boycott not
only the Morton company, but any firm
employing the services of that company.
It was reported Jast night that many
retail merchants were warned during the
day not to do business with the Morton
company under penalty of a boycott. An
. official of the company said when inter
viewed that there was no truth in the
story. He intimated that threats of the
kind had been made, but that the com
pany had r^-id no attention to them. He
could give no reason for the reported ac
tion. If it was taken, and moreover he had
no iear of the outcome as far as the busi
ness ol the company was concerned.
The Retail Delivery' Drivers 1 Union has
a grievance against the Morton Special
Delivery which threatens to develop into
a boycott of the latter concern.
The will of Mrs. Susan G. Tevis was
filed for probate with the County Clerk
yesterday. It will be remembered that
Mrs. Tevis was the sole beneficiary of
the will of the late Lloyd Tevis, whose
estate was appraised at approximately
$8,000,000. This entire estate Mrs. Tevis
gave to her children and grand
children during her lifetime with the ex
ception of certain personal property of
the value of about $20,000, and this is the
entire estate to be administred upon by
her executors. In deeding her property
Mrs. Tevis gave to her children the
chare allowed by law. .
By her will the whole estate with the
exception of a few legacies of jewelry
is left to ' her four surviving children,
Harry L. Tevis. William S. Tevis, Mar
garet T. Blanding, wife of Gordon Blancl
irg, and Louise T. Sharon, wife of Fred
erick -W. Sharon, who each receive one
fifth, the remaining one-fifth being left
in trust for Alice Boalt Tevis and Hugh
Tovis, the children of the late Hugh
Tevis, until each child attains the age of
25 years, when such interest is to become
theirs' absolutely. .
Florence L. Breckinridge and John C.
Breckinridge, the two children of Mrs.
Frederick W. Sharon, issue of her first
marriage, who were adopted by Mrs,
Tevis, ara considered for all the purposes
of the will as the children of Mrs.
Sharon, the same as though they had
never been adopted.
Harry L. Tevis and William S. Tevis
are named in the will as executors, and
also as trustees of Alice Boalt Tevis and
Hugh Tevis. They are empowered to
serve without bonds.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MAY 6, 1902.
NATOTELAX GAS EXPLODES
DEMOLISHING A BUILDING
The building was entirely demolished,
and the loss will be heavy. Escaping
natural gas is supposed to have caused
the explosion. As far as known no one
was killed outright. A search of the
ruins is being made. . - -
MARION, Ind., May 5.-A building on
South Broad street, occupied by Farns
ler's drug etore. Rowan's grocery, John
Darnell's saloon and Hudson & Otis' res
taurant, was demolished by natural gas
just before noon to-day. Injuring at least
a score of people. A number of them are
badly Injured.

KOTICK to the stockholders of The Risdon
Iron and Locomotive Works Is hereby given
that in pursuance of a resolution of the
Board of Directors of eaid corporation,
unanimously adopted at a meeting of said
board, held at the offlce of the corporation,
in the city and county of San Francisco.
State of California, on the 17th day of
March. 1902, a meeting of the stockholders
cf The Risdon Iron and Locomotive Works
â¢will he held at the office of the corporation.
No. 29S Steuart st, in the city and county of
San Francisco, State of California (the same
being the principal place ofsimsiness of said
corporation and the building where the Board
Â©r Directors usually meet), on WEDNES-
DAY, the 21st day of May. 1902. at the hour
of 11 o'clock a. m., for the purpose of con-
sidering and acting upon a proposition to
create a bonded Indebtedness of said corpo-
ration to the amount of six hundred thou-
sand (600,000) dollars. United States gold
coin, for the purpose of providing moneys
to purchase additional property to complete
the works and extend the business of the
corporation, and for legitimate and neces-
sary purposes. By order of the Board of
Directors.
(Signed.) AUGUSTUS TAYLOR,
Secretary of The Risdon Iron and Locomo-
tive Works.

SAN RAFAEL., May 5.â The man who
shot at C. Scolari In a saloon. at Tiburon
yesterday was David Carr, a discharged
sailor in the United States navy. Carr
was brought to this city to-day and ar
raigned before Justice Rodden, . on the
charge of assault- with intent*. to commit
murder. He was placed under $1000 bail.
Carr to-day displayed a Dewey medal for
bravery, and also had discharge papers,
showing that he was on the Baltimore at
the battle of Manila.
REDDING, May 5.â Joseph Dais, aged
60, whose father once owned the entire
Reading grant; his wife, aged 50; their
daughter-in-law, Mrs. Manuel Dais, and
her two young children, were driving into
Anderson thl3 afternoon. Their spirited
team became frightened when near the
railroad, ran away and made & complete
circuit of the town. Finally a wheel
struck a stone and Upset * the spring
wagon, Its five occupants being hurled
out in different directions. Mrs. Joseph
Dais was injured internally, and cannot
recover. â¢ The others escaped with slight
bruises. -

J. C. Cantwell of the United States rev
enue cutter service delivered an interest
ing address last night in the Academy of
Sciences auditorium on "The Alaskan Na
tives." He gave an instructive description
of their manners and- customs and of the
country which they occupy. ! He said that
the Indians of the Yukon Valley were
highly civilized, and that it was impera
tive that the United States Government
should take steps for their preservation
a* they were being rapidly crushed to the
wall by the white people of that vicious
and lawless class that always travel in
the van of new civilization.
"Would Preserve Ainsfcan Indians.
Diedrich von Duering, an old, wealthy
and hitherto respected citizen of Lark
mead, Napa County,' was placed on trial
yesterday before a Jury in the. United
States District Court on a charge of tak
ing out of the postofSce at Calistoga let
ters addressed to Mrs. Robert Preitz, the
wife of Mrs. von Duering's brother. Mr*.
Preitz was residing with the Von Duer
ings while her husband, a marine en
gineer, was at sea, and last summer sha
discovered, according to her # testimony
yesterday afternoon, that Von Duering
had been corresponding in her name -with
Walter Luhn at St. Louis and was receiv
ing arid . perusing the replies. When she
learned what had been going. on she com
plained to Chief Postofflce Inspector Rob
ert R. Munro, who detailed one of the
brightest men of his force. Inspector Den
nis Coyne, to secure the evidence.
Von Duering shortly before his arrest
made . a statement and affidavit to exon
erate himself. He said that Mrs. Preitz
when coming from Europe to New York
on her way to join her husband became
smitten with Mr. Luhn on the steamer,
and that at her request he (Von Duering)
wrote several love letters in German for
her, she not being able to write very
Â¦well in the German language. He re
ceived the replies and gave them to Mrs.
Preitz. She told Von Duering that she
had had a champagne supper and lots ot
fun with Mr. Luhn in New York, and sho
would like to have a little more fun In
carrying on a . correspondence, and that
she was too young to live buried on a
farm. Von Duering related how, when he
had. two of the letters in hl3 pocket, he
had asked Robert Preitz, the woman's
husband, how much would he give for
proofs of his wife's infidelity, and that
Preitz replied that it would be worth $1000
to him. Von Duering added that he dM
not tell the husband about the letters be
cause he was afraid that Preitz would
leave Mrs. Preitz on his hands.
Senator Gesf ord of Napa for the de
fense argued that the whole matter was
a "Josh." First Assistant United States
Attorney Banning denounced Von Duer
ing as a man destitute of moral sense,
and while he was doing so Mrs. von
Duering, his wife, broke into sobs, and It
was some time before she regained her
composure.
Judge de Haven will charge the Jury at
10 o'clock this morning.

The woman calling herself "Countess"
Ingorgolla and residing at 905 Ellis street
arid who stated last week that she was a

: George Raum, a prominent business
man and friend, of Findlayson, has peti
tioned the Superior Court .of Marin
County, asking to be appointed the guar
dian of the person and property of Find
layson, who has no relatives in this coun
try.- . .
Findlayson is 62 years of age and a na
tive of Scotland. He amassed a fortune
in the wholesale liquor business and has
vast real estate holdings in California.
About three weeks ago he was sudden
ly -stricken ill while enjoying a game of
bowling at San Rafael. His physicians
say he is suffering from a clot of blood
on the brain and is likely to die at any
moment.
Last week "Countess" Ingorgolia called
at the Hotel Rafael and claimed she was
Flndlayson's niece. She demanded the
key to his strong box and informed the
hotel manager that she would have her
"uncle" removed to her home in San
Francisco. The hotel manager refused to
give the woman the key to the box and
attending physicians declined to allow the
sick man to be moved from his rooms.
The "Countess" then declared that
Findlayson had made a will in her favor
and that A. C. Barry, claiming to be an
attorney and who resides in her flat at 905
Eddy street, had drawn it.
Raum was informed of the woman's
statement and on Saturday last filed a
petition for letters of guardianship.
Raum's attorneys are Dorn & Dora of
this city and Theodore J. Savage of San
Rafael. His legal advisers have "received
instructions to protect Findlayson's prop
erty from all persons representing them
selves as friends or relatives of the sick
man. Raum also intends to contest the
will alleged to have been drawn in favor
of the "Countess" should the woman pre
sent the document for probate in the event
of Findlayson's death.
The "Countess" Ingorgolia is not known
in local society's realm and she declines to
give Â¦ any information as to her ante
cedants. She is between 40 and 45 years of
age and not prepossessing.- ' '
A few years agd she, was known as Miss
Ingorgolia. to the persons to whom sie
rented rooms in a flat on Post. street. A.

thrown around the estate of James Find
layson, the capitalist, who is lying at the
point of death at the Hotel Rafael In San
Rafael, Marin County. ' '

An accident similar in nature occurred
at exactly the same place about three
months ago, in which four were injured
one seriously. _ â¢ '
Nobody happened to be sitting in the
open part of the Washington-street car
where it was struck by the car on Mason
street, as fatalities in that case would
have been almost unavoidable. The collid
ing car crushed in the steps of the other
one and broke the flooring and seat into
splinters. The heavy trucks on the Wash
ington-street car were bent and it was
necessary. to insert new trucks before the
car could be moved and the track cleared
Following the usual custom of street
car companies, the officials refused to give
any information' regarding the names- of
those injured or the extent of their in
juries. Six of those seriously hurt were
given " emergency treatment at a drug
store on the corner of Washington and
Mason streets, the proprietor of which
showed commendable willingness and un
usual skill in handling the wounded.
As quickly as possible a wrecking crew
was gathered and the i work of clearing
the line began. So badly were both cars
damaged that it took four hours of hard
work before the line was again open to
traffic. â¢ : â¢ Â¦
